          <a class="external text" href="http://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/yidd1255" rel="nofollow">
           yidd1255
          </a>
         </code>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glottolog_2-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Glottolog-2">
           [2]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">
          Linguasphere
         </a>
        </th>
        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">
         <code>
          52-ACB-g = 52-ACB-ga (West) + 52-ACB-gb (East); totalling 11 varieties
         </code>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Yiddish
       </b>
       (
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D7%99%D7%99%D6%B4%D7%93%D7%99%D7%A9" title="wikt:ייִדיש">
         ייִדיש
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D7%99%D7%99%D7%93%D7%99%D7%A9" title="wikt:יידיש">
         יידיש
        </a>
       </span>
       or
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D7%90%D7%99%D7%93%D7%99%D7%A9" title="wikt:אידיש">
         אידיש
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        yidish
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        idish
       </i>
       ,
       <abbr title="literal translation">
        lit.
       </abbr>
       "
       <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
        Jewish
       </a>
       ",
       <small>
        pronounced
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Yiddish" title="Help:IPA/Yiddish">
         [ˈjɪdɪʃ]
        </a>
       </span>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Yiddish" title="Help:IPA/Yiddish">
         [ˈɪdɪʃ]
        </a>
       </span>
       ; in older sources ייִדיש-טײַטש
       <i>
        Yidish-Taitsh
       </i>
       ,
       <abbr title="literal translation">
        lit.
       </abbr>
       Judaeo-German)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is the historical language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">
        Ashkenazi Jews
       </a>
       . It originated during the 9th century
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">
        Central Europe
       </a>
       , providing the nascent Ashkenazi community with a
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">
        High German
       </a>
       -based vernacular fused with elements taken from
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">
        Aramaic
       </a>
       as well as from
       <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">
        Slavic languages
       </a>
       and traces of
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Yiddish is written with a fully vocalized version of the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">
        Hebrew alphabet
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest surviving references date from the 12th century and call the language
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        לשון־אַשכּנז
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        loshn-ashknaz
       </i>
       , "language of Ashkenaz") or
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        טײַטש
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        taytsh
       </i>
       ), a variant of
       <i>
        tiutsch
       </i>
       , the contemporary name for
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German" title="Middle High German">
        Middle High German
       </a>
       . Colloquially, the language is sometimes called
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        מאַמע־לשון
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        mame-loshn
       </i>
       ,
       <abbr title="literal translation">
        lit.
       </abbr>
       "mother tongue"), distinguishing it from
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        לשון־קדש
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lashon_Hakodesh" title="Lashon Hakodesh">
         loshn koydesh
        </a>
       </i>
       , "holy tongue"), meaning Hebrew and Aramaic. The term "Yiddish", short for
       <i>
        Yidish Taitsh
       </i>
       "Jewish German", did not become the most frequently used designation in the literature until the 18th century. In the late 19th and into the 20th century the language was more commonly called "Jewish", especially in non-Jewish contexts, but "Yiddish" is again the more common designation.
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern Yiddish has
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_dialects" title="Yiddish dialects">
        two major forms
       </a>
       . Eastern Yiddish is far more common today. It includes Southeastern (Ukrainian–Romanian), Mideastern (Polish–Galician–Eastern Hungarian), and Northeastern (Lithuanian–Belarusian) dialects. Eastern Yiddish differs from Western both by its far greater size and by the extensive inclusion of words of Slavic origin. Western Yiddish is divided into Southwestern (Swiss–Alsatian–Southern German), Midwestern (Central German), and Northwestern (Netherlandic–Northern German) dialects. Yiddish is used in a number of
       <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">
        Orthodox Jewish
       </a>
       communities worldwide and is the first language of the home, school, and in many social settings among many
       <a href="/wiki/Haredi_Judaism" title="Haredi Judaism">
        Haredi Jews
       </a>
       , and is used in most
       <a href="/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism" title="Hasidic Judaism">
        Hasidic
       </a>
       and some
       <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_Jews" title="Lithuanian Jews">
        Lithuanian
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Yeshiva" title="Yeshiva">
        yeshivas
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The term Yiddish is also used in the adjectival sense, synonymously with Jewish, to designate attributes of
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddishkeit" title="Yiddishkeit">
        Yiddishkeit
       </a>
       ("Ashkenazi culture"; for example,
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_cuisine#Jewish_cuisine_variations" title="Jewish cuisine">
        Yiddish cooking
       </a>
       and "Yiddish music":
       <a href="/wiki/Klezmer" title="Klezmer">
        klezmer
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Prior to
       <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">
        the Holocaust
       </a>
       , there were 11—13 million speakers of Yiddish among 17 million Jews worldwide.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yivo-yiddish_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-yivo-yiddish-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       85% of the approximately 6 million Jews who died in the Holocaust were Yiddish speakers,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sprache_1984_p._3_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sprache_1984_p._3-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       leading to a massive decline in the use of the language. Assimilation following
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       further decreased the use of Yiddish both among survivors and Yiddish-speakers from other countries (such as in the Americas). However, the number of speakers is increasing in global Hasidic communities.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">
        Origins
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The established view is that, as with other Jewish languages, Jews speaking distinct languages learned new co-territorial vernaculars, which they then Judaized. In the case of Yiddish, this scenario sees it as emerging when speakers of
       <a href="/wiki/Zarphatic_language" title="Zarphatic language">
        Zarphatic
       </a>
       and other Judeo-Romance languages began to acquire varieties of
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German" title="Middle High German">
        Middle High German
       </a>
       , and from these groups the Ashkenazi community took shape.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ELL_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ELL-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spolsky_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spolsky-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Exactly what German base lies behind the earliest form of Yiddish is disputed.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Weinreich's model, speakers of
       <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">
        Old French
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Italian" title="Old Italian">
        Old Italian
       </a>
       , literate in Hebrew or Aramaic, migrated through Southern Europe to settle in the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhine_Valley" title="Rhine Valley">
        Rhine Valley
       </a>
       in an area known as
       <a href="/wiki/Lotharingia" title="Lotharingia">
        Lotharingia
       </a>
       (later known in Yiddish as
       <i>
        Loter
       </i>
       ) extending over parts of Germany and France;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There, they encountered and were influenced by Jewish speakers of
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">
        High German languages
       </a>
       and several other German dialects. Both Weinreich and
       <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Birnbaum" title="Solomon Birnbaum">
        Solomon Birnbaum
       </a>
       developed this model further in the mid-1950s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Weinreich's view, this Old Yiddish substrate later bifurcated into two distinct versions of the language, Western and Eastern Yiddish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleischer_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fleischer-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They retained the Semitic vocabulary and constructions needed for religious purposes and created a Judeo-German form of speech, sometimes not accepted as a fully autonomous language.
      </p>
      <p>
       Later linguistic research has finessed the Weinreich model or provided alternative approaches to the language's origins, with points of contention being the characterization of its Germanic base, the source of its Hebrew/Aramaic adstrata, and the means that and location where this fusion occurred. Some theorists argue that the fusion occurred with a Bavarian dialect base.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spolsky_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spolsky-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The two main candidates for the germinal matrix of Yiddish, the Rhineland and Bavaria, are not necessarily incompatible. There may have been parallel developments in the two regions, seeding the Western and Eastern dialects of Modern Yiddish.
       <a href="/wiki/Dovid_Katz" title="Dovid Katz">
        Dovid Katz
       </a>
       proposes that Yiddish emerged from contact between speakers of High German and Aramaic-speaking Jews from the Middle East.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yivo-yiddish_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-yivo-yiddish-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The lines of development proposed by the different theories do not necessarily rule out the others (at least not entirely); an article in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Forward" title="The Forward">
         The Forward
        </a>
       </i>
       argues that "in the end, a new 'standard theory' of Yiddish’s origins will probably be based on the work of Weinreich and his challengers alike."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wexler_(linguist)" title="Paul Wexler (linguist)">
        Paul Wexler
       </a>
       proposed a highly heterodox model in 1991 that took Yiddish, by which he means primarily eastern Yiddish,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleischer_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fleischer-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       not to be genetically grounded in a Germanic language at all, but rather as "Judeo-Sorbian" (a proposed Western Slavic language) whose lexicon had been
       <a href="/wiki/Relexification" title="Relexification">
        largely replaced
       </a>
       by High German in the 9th to 12th centuries, when, according to his theory, large numbers of German-speakers settled in
       <a href="/wiki/Sorbs" title="Sorbs">
        Sorbian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Polabian_language" title="Polabian language">
        Polabian
       </a>
       lands and developed
       <a href="/wiki/Knaanic_language" title="Knaanic language">
        Knaanic
       </a>
       , a Judeo-Slavic language which was subsequently relexified with Old High German.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spolsky_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spolsky-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In more recent work, Wexler has argued that Eastern Yiddish is unrelated genetically to Western Yiddish. Wexler's model has met with little academic support, and strong critical challenges, especially among historical linguists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spolsky_11-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spolsky-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleischer_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fleischer-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       By the 10th century, a distinctive Jewish culture had formed in
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">
        Central Europe
       </a>
       which came to be called
       <span lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        אַשכּנזי
       </span>
       <i>
        Ashkenazi
       </i>
       , "
       <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">
        Ashkenazi Jews
       </a>
       , from
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        אַשכּנז
       </span>
       ‎
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ashkenaz" title="Ashkenaz">
         Ashkenaz
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0110.htm#3" rel="nofollow">
        Genesis 10:3
       </a>
       ), the medieval
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_name" title="Hebrew name">
        Hebrew name
       </a>
       for northern Europe and
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ashkenaz was centered on the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">
        Rhineland
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Palatinate_(region)" title="Palatinate (region)">
        Palatinate
       </a>
       (notably
       <a href="/wiki/Worms,_Germany" title="Worms, Germany">
        Worms
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Speyer" title="Speyer">
        Speyer
       </a>
       ), in what is now the westernmost part of Germany. Its geographic extent did not coincide with the German principalities of the time, and it included northern
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       . Ashkenaz bordered on the area inhabited by another distinctive Jewish cultural group, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">
        Sephardi Jews
       </a>
       , who ranged into
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">
        southern France
       </a>
       . Ashkenazi culture later spread into
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">
        Eastern Europe
       </a>
       with large-scale population migrations.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Nothing is known with certainty about the
       <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">
        vernacular
       </a>
       of the earliest Jews in Germany, but several theories have been put forward. The first language of the Ashkenazim may, as noted above, have been the
       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">
        Aramaic language
       </a>
       , the vernacular of the Jews in
       <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">
        Roman-era Judea
       </a>
       and ancient and early medieval
       <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
        Mesopotamia
       </a>
       . The widespread use of Aramaic among the large non-Jewish
       <a href="/wiki/Syrians" title="Syrians">
        Syrian
       </a>
       trading population of the Roman provinces, including those in Europe, would have reinforced the use of Aramaic among Jews engaged in trade. In Roman times, many of the Jews living in
       <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">
        Rome
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">
        Southern Italy
       </a>
       appear to have been
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       -speakers, and this is reflected in some Ashkenazi personal names (e.g.,
       <i>
        Kalonymus
       </i>
       ). Hebrew, on the other hand, was regarded as a holy language reserved for ritual and spiritual purposes and not for common use. Much work needs to be done, though, to fully analyze the contributions of those languages to Yiddish.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is generally accepted that early Yiddish was likely to have contained elements from other languages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">
        Near East
       </a>
       and Europe, absorbed through migrations. Since some settlers may have come via France and Italy, it is also likely that the Romance-based Jewish languages of those regions were represented. Traces remain in the contemporary Yiddish vocabulary: for example,
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        בענטשן
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        bentshn
       </i>
       , "to bless"), from the Latin
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         benedicere
        </span>
       </i>
       ;
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        לייענען
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        leyenen
       </i>
       , "to read"), from the Latin
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         legere
        </span>
       </i>
       ; and the personal names
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        אַנשל
       </span>
       <i>
        Anshl
       </i>
       , cognate to Angel or Angelo;
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        בונים
       </span>
       Bunim (probably from "bon homme"). Western Yiddish includes additional words of Latin derivation (but still very few): for example,
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        אָרן
       </span>
       <i>
        orn
       </i>
       (to pray), cf. Latin and Italian "orare".
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Jewish community in the Rhineland would have encountered the many dialects from which
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
        Standard German
       </a>
       would emerge a few centuries later. In time, Jewish communities would have been speaking their own versions of these German dialects, mixed with linguistic elements that they themselves brought into the region. Although not reflected in the spoken language, a main point of difference was the use of the Hebrew alphabet for the recording of the Germanic vernacular, which may have been adopted either because of the community's familiarity with the alphabet or to prevent the non-Jewish population from understanding the correspondence. In addition, there was probably widespread illiteracy in the non-Hebrew script, with the level of illiteracy in the non-Jewish communities being even higher. Another point of difference was the use of Hebrew and Aramaic words. These words and terms were used because of their familiarity, but more so because in most cases there were no equivalent terms in the vernacular which could express the Jewish concepts or describe the objects of cultural significance.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Written_evidence">
        Written evidence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Written evidence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Machzor.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="1146" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Machzor.jpg/300px-Machzor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Machzor.jpg/450px-Machzor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Machzor.jpg/600px-Machzor.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Machzor.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The calligraphic segment in the Worms Mahzor
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       It is not known when
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_orthography" title="Yiddish orthography">
        Yiddish orthography
       </a>
       first developed. The oldest surviving literary document using it is a blessing in the Worms machzor,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a Hebrew prayer book from 1272. There is a scalable image online at the indicated reference. The Worms machzor is discussed in Frakes, 2004, and Baumgarten, ed. Frakes, 2005 – see the
       <i>
        Bibliography
       </i>
       at the foot of this article.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Yiddish
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
          גוּט טַק אִים בְּטַגְֿא שְ וַיר דִּיש מַחֲזוֹר אִין בֵּיתֿ הַכְּנֶסֶתֿ טְרַגְֿא
         </span>
         ‬
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Transliterated
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          gut tak im betage se vaer dis makhazor in beis hakneses trage
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Translated
        </th>
        <td>
         May a good day come to him who carries this prayer book into the synagogue.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       This brief rhyme is decoratively embedded in an otherwise purely Hebrew text.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nonetheless, it indicates that the Yiddish of that day was a more or less regular Middle High German written in the Hebrew alphabet into which Hebrew words –
       <span lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
         מַחֲזוֹר
        </span>
        ‬
       </span>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Machzor" title="Machzor">
        <i>
         makhazor
        </i>
       </a>
       (prayerbook for the
       <a href="/wiki/High_Holy_Days" title="High Holy Days">
        High Holy Days
       </a>
       ) and
       <span lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        בֵּיתֿ הַכְּנֶסֶתֿ
       </span>
       , "
       <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">
        synagogue
       </a>
       " (read in Yiddish as
       <i>
        beis hakneses
       </i>
       ) –had been included. The
       <a href="/wiki/Niqqud" title="Niqqud">
        niqqud
       </a>
       appears as though it might have been added by a second scribe, in which case it may need to be dated separately and may not be indicative of the pronunciation of the rhyme at the time of its initial annotation.
      </p>
      <p>
       Over the course of the 14th and 15th centuries, songs and poems in Yiddish, and
       <a href="/wiki/Macaronic_language" title="Macaronic language">
        macaronic
       </a>
       pieces in Hebrew and German, began to appear. These were collected in the late 15th century by Menahem ben Naphtali Oldendorf.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During the same period, a tradition seems to have emerged of the Jewish community's adapting its own versions of German secular literature. The earliest Yiddish epic poem of this sort is the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dukus_Horant" title="Dukus Horant">
         Dukus Horant
        </a>
       </i>
       , which survives in the famous Cambridge Codex T.-S.10.K.22. This 14th-century manuscript was discovered in the
       <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Geniza" title="Cairo Geniza">
        Cairo Geniza
       </a>
       in 1896, and also contains a collection of narrative poems on themes from the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">
        Hebrew Bible
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Haggadah" title="Haggadah">
        Haggadah
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Printing">
        Printing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Printing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The advent of the
       <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">
        printing press
       </a>
       in the 16th century enabled the large scale production of works, at a cheaper cost, some of which have survived. One particularly popular work was
       <a href="/wiki/Elia_Levita" title="Elia Levita">
        Elia Levita
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bovo-Bukh" title="Bovo-Bukh">
         Bovo-Bukh
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        בָּבָֿא-בּוך
       </span>
       ), composed around 1507–08 and printed in at least forty editions, beginning in 1518 (Pesaro). Levita, the earliest named Yiddish author, may also have written
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        פּאַריז און װיענע
       </span>
       <i>
        Pariz un Viene
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">
        Paris
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">
        Vienna
       </a>
       ). Another Yiddish retelling of a chivalric romance, װידװילט
       <i>
        Vidvilt
       </i>
       (often referred to as "Widuwilt" by Germanizing scholars), presumably also dates from the 15th century, although the manuscripts are from the 16th. It is also known as
       <i>
        Kinig Artus Hof
       </i>
       , an adaptation of the Middle High German romance
       <i>
        Wigalois
       </i>
       by Wirnt von Gravenberg.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another significant writer is Avroham ben Schemuel Pikartei, who published a paraphrase on the
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Job" title="Book of Job">
        Book of Job
       </a>
       in 1557.
      </p>
      <p>
       Women in the Ashkenazi community were traditionally not literate in Hebrew, but did read and write Yiddish. A body of literature therefore developed for which women were a primary audience. This included secular works, such as the
       <i>
        Bovo-Bukh
       </i>
       , and religious writing specifically for women, such as the
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        צאנה וראינה
       </span>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tseno_Ureno" title="Tseno Ureno">
         Tseno Ureno
        </a>
       </i>
       and the
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        תחנות
       </span>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tkhine" title="Tkhine">
         Tkhines
        </a>
       </i>
       . One of the best-known early woman authors was
       <a href="/wiki/Gl%C3%BCckel_of_Hameln" title="Glückel of Hameln">
        Glückel of Hameln
       </a>
       , whose memoirs are still in print.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Page_from_Yiddish-Hebrew-Latin-German_dictionary_by_Elijah_Levita.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="622" data-file-width="432" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Page_from_Yiddish-Hebrew-Latin-German_dictionary_by_Elijah_Levita.jpg/220px-Page_from_Yiddish-Hebrew-Latin-German_dictionary_by_Elijah_Levita.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Page_from_Yiddish-Hebrew-Latin-German_dictionary_by_Elijah_Levita.jpg/330px-Page_from_Yiddish-Hebrew-Latin-German_dictionary_by_Elijah_Levita.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Page_from_Yiddish-Hebrew-Latin-German_dictionary_by_Elijah_Levita.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Page_from_Yiddish-Hebrew-Latin-German_dictionary_by_Elijah_Levita.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A page from the
         <a class="external text" href="http://cf.uba.uva.nl/nl/publicaties/treasures/page/p08.html" rel="nofollow">
          <i>
           Shemot Devarim
          </i>
         </a>
         (
         <abbr title="literal translation">
          lit.
         </abbr>
         <i>
          Names of Things
         </i>
         ), a Yiddish–Hebrew–Latin–German dictionary and thesaurus, published by Elia Levita in 1542
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The segmentation of the Yiddish readership, between women who read
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        מאַמע־לשון
       </span>
       <i>
        mame-loshn
       </i>
       but not
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        לשון־קדש
       </span>
       <i>
        loshn-koydesh
       </i>
       , and men who read both, was significant enough that distinctive
       <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">
        typefaces
       </a>
       were used for each. The name commonly given to the semicursive form used exclusively for Yiddish was
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        ווײַבערטײַטש
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        vaybertaytsh
       </i>
       = "women's
       <i>
        taytsh
       </i>
       ," shown in the heading and fourth column in the adjacent illustration), with square Hebrew letters (shown in the third column) being reserved for text in that language and Aramaic. This distinction was retained in general typographic practice through to the early 19th century, with Yiddish books being set in
       <i>
        vaybertaytsh
       </i>
       (also termed
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        מעשייט
       </span>
       <i>
        mesheyt
       </i>
       or
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        מאַשקעט
       </span>
       <i>
        mashket
       </i>
       —the construction is uncertain).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An additional distinctive semicursive typeface was, and still is, used for rabbinical commentary on religious texts when Hebrew and Yiddish appear on the same page. This is commonly termed
       <a href="/wiki/Rashi_script" title="Rashi script">
        Rashi script
       </a>
       , from the name of the most renowned early author, whose commentary is usually printed using this script. (Rashi is also the typeface normally used when the Sephardic counterpart to Yiddish,
       <a href="/wiki/Judaeo-Spanish" title="Judaeo-Spanish">
        Judaeo-Spanish
       </a>
       or
       <i>
        Ladino
       </i>
       , is printed in Hebrew script.)
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Secularization">
        Secularization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Secularization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Western Yiddish dialect—sometimes pejoratively labeled
       <i>
        Mauscheldeutsch
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (i. e. "Moses German")
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       —declined in the 18th century, as the
       <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">
        Age of Enlightenment
       </a>
       and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Haskalah" title="Haskalah">
         Haskalah
        </a>
       </i>
       led to a view of Yiddish as a corrupt dialect. A
       <a href="/wiki/Maskil" title="Maskil">
        Maskil
       </a>
       (from the same root word as Haskalah) would write about and promote acclimatization to the outside world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Jewish children began attending secular schools where the primary language spoken and taught was German, not Yiddish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Owing to both assimilation to German and the revival of
       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew" title="Modern Hebrew">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       , Western Yiddish survived only as a language of "intimate family circles or of closely knit trade groups". (
       <a href="#liptzin1972">
        Liptzin 1972
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       In eastern Europe, the response to these forces took the opposite direction, with Yiddish becoming the cohesive force in a
       <a href="/wiki/Secularity" title="Secularity">
        secular culture
       </a>
       (see the
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddishist_movement" title="Yiddishist movement">
        Yiddishist movement
       </a>
       ). Notable Yiddish writers of the late 19th and early 20th centuries are Sholem Yankev Abramovitch, writing as
       <a href="/wiki/Mendele_Mocher_Sforim" title="Mendele Mocher Sforim">
        Mendele Mocher Sforim
       </a>
       ; Sholem Rabinovitsh, widely known as
       <a href="/wiki/Sholem_Aleichem" title="Sholem Aleichem">
        Sholem Aleichem
       </a>
       , whose stories about
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        טבֿיה דער מילכיקער
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        Tevye der milkhiker
       </i>
       , "
       <a href="/wiki/Tevye" title="Tevye">
        Tevye
       </a>
       the Dairyman") inspired the Broadway musical and film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fiddler_on_the_Roof" title="Fiddler on the Roof">
         Fiddler on the Roof
        </a>
       </i>
       ; and
       <a href="/wiki/I._L._Peretz" title="I. L. Peretz">
        Isaac Leib Peretz
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">
        20th century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 20th century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yiddish_WWI_poster2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3880" data-file-width="2564" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Yiddish_WWI_poster2.jpg/220px-Yiddish_WWI_poster2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Yiddish_WWI_poster2.jpg/330px-Yiddish_WWI_poster2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Yiddish_WWI_poster2.jpg/440px-Yiddish_WWI_poster2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yiddish_WWI_poster2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
          American
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
          World War I
         </a>
         -era poster in Yiddish. Translated caption: "Food will win the war – You came here seeking freedom, now you must help to preserve it – We must supply the
         <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">
          Allies
         </a>
         with wheat – Let nothing go to waste". Colour lithograph, 1917. Digitally restored.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the early 20th century, especially after the Socialist
       <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">
        October Revolution
       </a>
       in Russia, Yiddish was emerging as a major Eastern European language. Its rich literature was more widely published than ever,
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_theatre" title="Yiddish theatre">
        Yiddish theatre
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_cinema" title="Yiddish cinema">
        Yiddish cinema
       </a>
       were booming, and it for a time achieved status as one of the official languages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_People%27s_Republic" title="Ukrainian People's Republic">
        Ukrainian People's Republic
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">
        Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       and the short-lived
       <a href="/wiki/Galician_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Galician Soviet Socialist Republic">
        Galician Soviet Socialist Republic
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Autonomous_Oblast" title="Jewish Autonomous Oblast">
        Jewish Autonomous Oblast
       </a>
       . Educational autonomy for Jews in several countries (notably
       <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">
        Poland
       </a>
       ) after
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       led to an increase in formal Yiddish-language education, more uniform orthography, and to the 1925 founding of the Yiddish Scientific Institute,
       <a href="/wiki/YIVO" title="YIVO">
        YIVO
       </a>
       . In
       <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">
        Vilnius
       </a>
       , there was debate over which language should take primacy, Hebrew or Yiddish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Yiddish changed significantly during the 20th century.
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Wex" title="Michael Wex">
        Michael Wex
       </a>
       writes, "As increasing numbers of Yiddish speakers moved from the Slavic-speaking East to Western Europe and the Americas in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, they were so quick to jettison Slavic vocabulary that the most prominent Yiddish writers of the time—the founders of modern Yiddish literature, who were still living in Slavic-speaking countries—revised the printed editions of their oeuvres to eliminate obsolete and 'unnecessary' Slavisms."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The vocabulary used in Israel absorbed many Modern Hebrew words, and there was a similar but smaller increase in the English component of Yiddish in the United States and, to a lesser extent, the United Kingdom.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       This has resulted in some difficulty in communication between Yiddish speakers from Israel and those from other countries.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_phonology" title="Yiddish phonology">
        Yiddish phonology
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Yiddish phonology is similar to
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German_phonology" title="Standard German phonology">
        that of Standard German
       </a>
       . However, it does not have
       <a href="/wiki/Final-obstruent_devoicing" title="Final-obstruent devoicing">
        final-obstruent devoicing
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fortis_and_lenis" title="Fortis and lenis">
        fortis
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">
        voiceless
       </a>
       )
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
        stop consonants
       </a>
       are unaspirated, and the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /χ/
       </span>
       phoneme is invariably uvular, unlike the German phoneme
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /x/
       </span>
       , which is palatal, velar, or uvular.
      </p>
      <p>
       Yiddish has a smaller inventory of
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
        vowels
       </a>
       than Standard German and no
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">
        vowel length
       </a>
       distinction.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers_of_speakers">
        Numbers of speakers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Numbers of speakers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yiddish.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="600" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Yiddish.png/220px-Yiddish.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Yiddish.png/330px-Yiddish.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Yiddish.png/440px-Yiddish.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yiddish.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map of the Yiddish dialects between the 15th and the 19th centuries (Western dialects in orange / Eastern dialects in green).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On the eve of
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       , there were 11 to 13 million Yiddish speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yivo-yiddish_8-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-yivo-yiddish-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">
        The Holocaust
       </a>
       , however, led to a dramatic, sudden decline in the use of Yiddish, as the extensive Jewish communities, both secular and religious, that used Yiddish in their day-to-day life, were largely destroyed. Around five million of those killed — 85 percent of the Jews who died in the Holocaust — were speakers of Yiddish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sprache_1984_p._3_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sprache_1984_p._3-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although millions of Yiddish speakers survived the war (including nearly all Yiddish speakers in the Americas), further assimilation in countries such as the United States and the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       , along with the strictly monolingual stance of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zionist" title="Zionist">
        Zionist
       </a>
       movement, led to a decline in the use of Eastern Yiddish. However, the number of speakers within the widely dispersed Orthodox (mainly Hasidic) communities is now increasing. Although used in various countries, Yiddish has attained official recognition as a minority language only in
       <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">
        Moldova
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">
        Bosnia and Herzegovina
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
        Netherlands
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">
        Sweden
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Reports of the number of current Yiddish speakers vary significantly.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
         Ethnologue
        </a>
       </i>
       estimates, based on publications through 1991, that there were at that time 1.5 million speakers of Eastern Yiddish,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of which 40% lived in Ukraine, 15% in Israel, and 10% in the United States. The
       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Language_Association" title="Modern Language Association">
        Modern Language Association
       </a>
       agrees with fewer than 200,000 in the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Western Yiddish is reported by
       <i>
        Ethnologue
       </i>
       to have had an ethnic population of 50,000 in 2000, and an undated speaking population of 5,000, mostly in Germany.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-western_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-western-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A 1996 report by the
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">
        Council of Europe
       </a>
       estimates a worldwide Yiddish-speaking population of about two million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Further
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics" title="Demographics">
        demographic
       </a>
       information about the recent status of what is treated as an Eastern–Western dialect continuum is provided in the YIVO
       <i>
        Language and Cultural Atlas of Ashkenazic Jewry
       </i>
       (Language and Cultural Atlas of Ashkenazic Jewry).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Status_as_a_language">
        Status as a language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Status as a language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       There has been frequent debate about the extent of the linguistic independence of Yiddish from the languages that it absorbed. There has been periodic assertion that Yiddish is a dialect of German, or even "just broken German, more of a linguistic mishmash than a true language".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even when recognized as an autonomous language, it has sometimes been referred to as Judeo-German, along the lines of other Jewish languages like
       <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Persian" title="Judeo-Persian">
        Judeo-Persian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Judaeo-Spanish" title="Judaeo-Spanish">
        Judaeo-Spanish
       </a>
       or Zarphatic. A widely cited summary of attitudes in the 1930s was published by
       <a href="/wiki/Max_Weinreich" title="Max Weinreich">
        Max Weinreich
       </a>
       , quoting a remark by an auditor of one of his lectures:
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        אַ שפּראַך איז אַ דיאַלעקט מיט אַן אַרמיי און פֿלאָט
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        a shprakh iz a dialekt mit an armey un flot
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       — "
       <a href="/wiki/A_language_is_a_dialect_with_an_army_and_navy" title="A language is a dialect with an army and navy">
        A language is a dialect with an army and navy
       </a>
       ").
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Israel_and_Zionism">
        Israel and Zionism
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Israel and Zionism">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yidish_graffiti.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2266" data-file-width="1672" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yidish_graffiti.JPG/220px-Yidish_graffiti.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yidish_graffiti.JPG/330px-Yidish_graffiti.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yidish_graffiti.JPG/440px-Yidish_graffiti.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yidish_graffiti.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An example of graffiti in Yiddish, Tel Aviv, Washington Avenue (און איר זאלט ליב האבן דעם פרעמדען, ווארום פרעמדע זייט איר געווען אין לאנד מצרים
         <i>
          Un ir zolt lib hobn dem fremdn varum fremde seit ir geven in land mitsrayim
         </i>
         ).
         <span lang="en" xml:lang="en">
          "Ye shalt neither vex a stranger, nor oppress him: for ye were strangers in the land of Egypt (Exodus 22:21) Amen?"
         </span>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The national languages of Israel are Hebrew and Arabic. The debate in Zionist circles over the use of Yiddish in Israel and in the Diaspora in preference to Hebrew also reflected the tensions between religious and secular Jewish lifestyles. Many secular Zionists wanted Hebrew as the sole language of Jews, to contribute to a national cohesive identity. Traditionally religious Jews, on the other hand, preferred use of Yiddish, viewing Hebrew as a respected holy language reserved for prayer and religious study. In the early 20th century, Zionist activists in
       <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">
        Palestine
       </a>
       tried to eradicate the use of Yiddish among Jews in preference to Hebrew, and make its use socially unacceptable.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This conflict also reflected the opposing views among secular Jews worldwide, one side seeing Hebrew (and Zionism) and the other Yiddish (and
       <a href="/wiki/Internationalism_(politics)" title="Internationalism (politics)">
        Internationalism
       </a>
       ) as the means of defining Jewish nationalism. In the 1920s and 1930s,
       <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
        גדוד מגיני השפה
       </span>
       ‬
       <i>
        gdud maginéi hasafá
       </i>
       , "the language defendants regiment", whose motto was "
       <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
        עברי, דבר עברית
       </span>
       ‬
       <i>
        ivri, dabér ivrít
       </i>
       ," that is, "Hebrew [i.e. Jew], speak Hebrew!", used to tear down signs written in "foreign" languages and disturb Yiddish theatre gatherings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, according to linguist
       <a href="/wiki/Ghil%27ad_Zuckermann" title="Ghil'ad Zuckermann">
        Ghil'ad Zuckermann
       </a>
       , the members of this group in particular, and the Hebrew revival in general, did not succeed in uprooting Yiddish patterns (as well as the patterns of other European languages Jewish immigrants spoke) within what he calls "Israeli", i.e.
       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew" title="Modern Hebrew">
        Modern Hebrew
       </a>
       . Zuckermann believes that "Israeli does include numerous Hebrew elements resulting from a conscious revival but also numerous pervasive linguistic features deriving from a subconscious survival of the revivalists’ mother tongues, e.g. Yiddish."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After the founding of the State of Israel, a massive wave of
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_exodus_from_Arab_and_Muslim_countries" title="Jewish exodus from Arab and Muslim countries">
        Jewish immigrants from Arab countries
       </a>
       arrived. In short order, these
       <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi_Jews" title="Mizrahi Jews">
        Mizrahi Jews
       </a>
       and their descendants would account for nearly half the Jewish population. While all were at least familiar with Hebrew as a liturgical language, essentially none had any contact with or affinity for Yiddish (some, of
       <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">
        Sephardic
       </a>
       origin, spoke Judaeo-Spanish, others various
       <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Arabic_languages" title="Judeo-Arabic languages">
        Judeo-Arabic languages
       </a>
       ). Thus, Hebrew emerged as the dominant linguistic common denominator between the different population groups.
      </p>
      <p>
       In religious circles, it is the Ashkenazi
       <a href="/wiki/Haredi_Judaism" title="Haredi Judaism">
        Haredi Jews
       </a>
       , particularly the Hasidic Jews and the Lithuanian yeshiva world (see
       <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_Jews" title="Lithuanian Jews">
        Lithuanian Jews
       </a>
       ), who continue to teach, speak and use Yiddish, making it a language used regularly by hundreds of thousands of Haredi Jews today. The largest of these centers are in
       <a href="/wiki/Bnei_Brak" title="Bnei Brak">
        Bnei Brak
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
        Jerusalem
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       There is a growing revival of interest in Yiddish culture among secular Israelis, with the flourishing of new proactive cultural organizations like YUNG YiDiSH, as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_theatre" title="Yiddish theatre">
        Yiddish theatre
       </a>
       (usually with simultaneous translation to Hebrew and Russian) and young people are taking university courses in Yiddish, some achieving considerable fluency.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Former_Soviet_Union">
        Former Soviet Union
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Former Soviet Union">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the Soviet Union during the 1920s, Yiddish was promoted as the language of the Jewish
       <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">
        proletariat
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_SSR_(1927).svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="787" data-file-width="758" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_SSR_%281927%29.svg/220px-Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_SSR_%281927%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_SSR_%281927%29.svg/330px-Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_SSR_%281927%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_SSR_%281927%29.svg/440px-Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_SSR_%281927%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_SSR_(1927).svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         State emblem of the
         <a href="/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">
          Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic
         </a>
         with the motto
         <i>
          Workers of the world, unite!
         </i>
         in Yiddish (lower left part of the ribbon)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       It was one of the official languages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">
        Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic
       </a>
       . Until 1938, the
       <a href="/wiki/Emblem_of_the_Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Emblem of the Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">
        Emblem of the Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic
       </a>
       included the motto
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Workers_of_the_world,_unite!" title="Workers of the world, unite!">
         Workers of the world, unite!
        </a>
       </i>
       in Yiddish. Yiddish was also official language in several agricultural districts of the
       <a href="/wiki/Galician_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Galician Soviet Socialist Republic">
        Galician Soviet Socialist Republic
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A public educational system entirely based on the Yiddish language was established and comprised kindergartens, schools, and higher educational institutions (technical schools,
       <a href="/wiki/Rabfak" title="Rabfak">
        rabfaks
       </a>
       and other university departments). At the same time, Hebrew was considered a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bourgeois" title="Bourgeois">
        bourgeois
       </a>
       language and its use was generally discouraged. The vast majority of the Yiddish-language cultural institutions were closed in the late 1930s along with cultural institutions of other ethnic minorities lacking administrative entities of their own. The last Yiddish-language schools, theaters and publications were closed by the end of the 1940s. It continued to be spoken widely for decades, nonetheless, in areas with compact Jewish populations (primarily in Moldova, Ukraine, and to a lesser extent Belarus).
      </p>
      <p>
       In the former Soviet states, recently active Yiddish authors include Yoysef Burg (
       <a href="/wiki/Chernivtsi" title="Chernivtsi">
        Chernivtsi
       </a>
       1912–2009) and
       <a href="/wiki/Olexander_Beyderman" title="Olexander Beyderman">
        Olexander Beyderman
       </a>
       (b. 1949,
       <a href="/wiki/Odessa" title="Odessa">
        Odessa
       </a>
       ). Publication of an earlier Yiddish periodical (
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        דער פֿרײַנד
       </span>
       - der fraynd; lit. "The Friend"), was resumed in 2004 with
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        דער נײַער פֿרײַנד
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        der nayer fraynd
       </i>
       ; lit. "The New Friend",
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">
        Saint Petersburg
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">
        Russia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Russia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       According to the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Census_(2010)" title="Russian Census (2010)">
        2010 census
       </a>
       , 1,683 people spoke Yiddish in Russia, approximately 1% of all the Jews of the Russian Federation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Shvydkoy" title="Mikhail Shvydkoy">
        Mikhail Shvydkoy
       </a>
       , former Minister of Culture of Russia and himself of Jewish origin, Yiddish culture in Russia is gone, and its revival is unlikely.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lechaim.ru_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lechaim.ru-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        From my point of view, Yiddish culture today isn't just fading away, but disappearing. It is stored as memories, as fragments of phrases, as books that have long gone unread. ... Yiddish culture is dying and this should be treated with utmost calm. There is no need to pity that which cannot be resurrected — it has receded into the world of the enchanting past, where it should remain. Any artificial culture, a culture without replenishment, is meaningless. ... Everything that happens with Yiddish culture is transformed into a kind of cabaret—epistolary genre, nice, cute to the ear and the eye, but having nothing to do with high art, because there is no natural, national soil. In Russia, it is the memory of the departed, sometimes sweet memories. But it's the memories of what will never be again. Perhaps that's why these memories are always so sharp.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lechaim.ru_40-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Lechaim.ru-40">
          [40]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_Autonomous_Oblast">
        Jewish Autonomous Oblast
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Jewish Autonomous Oblast">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RussiaJewish2007-07.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="178" data-file-width="300" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/RussiaJewish2007-07.png/220px-RussiaJewish2007-07.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/RussiaJewish2007-07.png 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RussiaJewish2007-07.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Jewish_Autonomous_Oblast" title="History of the Jews in the Jewish Autonomous Oblast">
          Jewish Autonomous Oblast
         </a>
         in the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">
          USSR
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Autonomous_Oblast" title="Jewish Autonomous Oblast">
        Jewish Autonomous Oblast
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Birobidzhan" title="Birobidzhan">
        Birobidzhan
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Jewish_Autonomous_Oblast" title="History of the Jews in the Jewish Autonomous Oblast">
        History of the Jews in the Jewish Autonomous Oblast
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Jewish Autonomous Oblast was formed in 1934 in the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">
        Russian Far East
       </a>
       , with its capital city in Birobidzhan and Yiddish as its official language. The intention was for the Soviet Jewish population to settle there. Jewish cultural life was revived in Birobidzhan much earlier than elsewhere in the Soviet Union. Yiddish theaters began opening in the 1970s. The newspaper
       <span lang="yi" xml:lang="yi">
        דער ביראָבידזשאַנער שטערן
       </span>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Birobidzhaner_Shtern" title="Birobidzhaner Shtern">
        <i>
         Der Birobidzhaner Shtern
        </i>
       </a>
       ; lit: "The Birobidzhan Star") includes a Yiddish section.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although the official status of the language was not retained by the Russian Federation, its cultural significance is still recognized and bolstered. The First Birobidzhan International Summer Program for Yiddish Language and Culture was launched in 2007.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As of 2010
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , according to data provided by the Russian Census Bureau, there were 97 speakers of Yiddish in the JAO.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ukraine">
        Ukraine
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ukraine">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Yiddish was an official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_People%27s_Republic" title="Ukrainian People's Republic">
        Ukrainian People's Republic
       </a>
       (1917–21).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ukraine:_Birth_of_a_Modern_Nation_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ukraine:_Birth_of_a_Modern_Nation-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HoUJsntui_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HoUJsntui-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Council_of_Europe">
        Council of Europe
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Council of Europe">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Several countries which ratified the 1992
       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">
        European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages
       </a>
       have included Yiddish in the list of their recognized minority languages: the Netherlands (1996), Sweden (2000), Romania (2008), Poland (2009), Bosnia and Herzegovina (2010). In 2005, Ukraine did not mention Yiddish as such, but "the language(s) of the Jewish ethnic minority".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">
        Sweden
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yiddish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sweden">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jidische.Folkschtime.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1371" data-file-width="2209" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Jidische.Folkschtime.jpg/220px-Jidische.Folkschtime.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Jidische.Folkschtime.jpg/330px-Jidische.Folkschtime.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Jidische.Folkschtime.jpg/440px-Jidische.Folkschtime.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jidische.Folkschtime.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Banner from the first issue of the
         <i>
          Yidishe Folksshtime
         </i>
         ("Yiddish People's Voice"), published in Stockholm, 12 January 1917.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In June 1999, the Swedish Parliament enacted legislation giving Yiddish legal status
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as one of the country's
       <a href="/wiki/Official_minority_languages_of_Sweden" title="Official minority languages of Sweden">
        official minority languages
       </a>
       (entering into effect in April 2000). The rights thereby conferred are not detailed, but additional legislation was enacted in June 2006 establishing a new governmental agency, The Swedish National Language Council,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the mandate of which instructs it to "collect, preserve, scientifically research, and spread material about the national minority languages", naming them all explicitly, including Yiddish.
